          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780717803019" href="/book/show/10494763-german-ideology">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2328172236" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1582396457245392713">Insightful, funny and Marx at his best. A great starter for people who want to understand his work and ideas. </span>

<div id="review_2062140142" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2062140142" />
    <a title="Andre" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17422382-andre"><img alt="Andre" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1500234428p2/17422382.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2062140142?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andre" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andre" href="/user/show/17422382-andre">Andre</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780853152170" href="/book/show/797497.German_Ideology">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2062140142" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12258048987984949411">Written in 1846, unpublished till 1932 in Soviet Union. For the first time in this book Marx & Engels (i would say Marx&Engels with subject He, because i think they both can not be divided with one another, they both are indeed an appearance of dialectic) explained materialistic conception of history and emphasized communism as deterministic society. His criticism devastated German Idealism which had been at the time dominant in Prussia, especially Hegelianism and of course, the man who heavily </span>
  <span id="freeText12258048987984949411" style="display:none">Written in 1846, unpublished till 1932 in Soviet Union. For the first time in this book Marx &amp; Engels (i would say Marx&amp;Engels with subject He, because i think they both can not be divided with one another, they both are indeed an appearance of dialectic) explained materialistic conception of history and emphasized communism as deterministic society. His criticism devastated German Idealism which had been at the time dominant in Prussia, especially Hegelianism and of course, the man who heavily influenced Marx's understanding on philosophy of materialism, Ludwig Feuerbach. His thinking was also influenced by Hegel, mainly dialectical method. Hegels's historical conception defined history as development of human's principal and in parallel with consciousness (human's reason) and he defined the essence of human was working, bcs everything is occured by creation of human and it derrived from consiousness which inherently, naturally, divinely human has. State (based on freedom) are appearance of a development human's consciousness in history through the consiousness which before the emergence of state alienated relation between people upon each other. State is the highest result of its dialectical process between Master and Serf, for instance he said in Phänomenologie des Geistes and in Enzyklopädie that master (who is literally subject, too) is actually not free by treating another subject (serf) badly. This alienation can only be abolished through common consciousness (Allgemeines Bewusstsein), State of law is the inevitably progress of reason/consciousness : <br /><br />"Durch die Arbeit kommt (das Bewußtsein) zu sich selbst. In dem Moment, welches der Begierde im Bewußtsein des Herrn entspricht, schien dem dienenden Bewußtsein zwar die Seite der unwesentlichen Beziehung (Entfremdung) auf das Ding zugefallen zu sein, indem das Ding darin eine Selbständigkeit behält."<br />"Das... Resultat des Kampfes im Anerkennung (zwischen Herr und Knecht) ist das.. Allgemeine Selbstbewußtsein (common consciousness), d.h dasjenige freie Selbstbewußten nicht mehr... ein unfreies, sondern ein gleichfalls selbständiges ist."<br /><br />These opinions are point of His (Marx&amp;Engels) criticism, <br />He opined in totally different way of thinking He satirically then criticized :<br /><br />"In direct contrast to German Philosophy which descends from heaven to earth, here we ascend from earth to heaven. That is to say, we do not set out from what men say, imagine, conceive, nor from men as narrated, thought of, imagined, conceived, in order to arrive at men in the flesh." <br /> (The German Ideology, edited&amp;introduced by C.J Arthur, p.47)<br /><br />Men's history is not merely driven by consciousness, men's history is driven by object surrounding and economic situation which will determine how men thinks and shapes the world after his own image, consciousness is determined by life/Not subject but object where everything began, not purely consciousness itself. Then to Feuerbach (although he confessed as a materialist philosopher) who distinguished men and animal by being sure that men has no limited character as animal, like reason and religion without arguing where that reason comes from, as if it was inevitably grace of god. Religion which according to Feuerbach a product of embedded individual, but it is a product of society in which it depends on basis. His prime historical materialism is the answer of all this fallacy of German Idealism which had problem with reality. Historical materialism analyses the genealogie of private eigentum (and its division of labour) and proves that material needs (Gegenstände, Object) had a great desicive role as driving force of history instead of consciousness. He explained firstly early communist society in which private property did not exist and hierarchy was merely in family or clan chief, then due to growing population emerged inevitably wars, conquest and slavery. "Then modern private property appeared only on a more extensive scale (concentration of private property) which began very early in Rome (proved by Licinian agrarian law 367 B.C)". In Rome which highly civilised, "had developed productive forces and after that in early middle ages european tribes resisted against roman colonial and destroying a number of productive force. Agriculture had declined, industry had decayed for want of a market, trade had died out or been violently suspended, the rural and urban population had been decreased. In such conditions and the mode of organisation of the conquest determined by them and feudal property appeared as the third ownership". In feudalism merchant (bourgeoisie) demanded fairness in trade which nobles had at the time normally restricted and burdened them taxes. Social relation between feudal and merchant was abolished through American Revolution 1776 and French Revolution 1789. The era of proletarianism emerged then. He tried to depict society as a whole through economic condition- relation between mode of production &amp; socio-politic are undivideable.<br /><br />German philosophy (Idealism) has landed in a wrong direction, well flawed, in a wrong consciousness (verkehrtes Bewußtsein) and this is actually a regression for Him, this has completely alienated human being between his fellow as well as to its real world. This point of view must be replaced and restored by scientific worldview which is also communism. A very interesting example i got from the class :<br /><br />"Our development is our own deed" - secularised version (scientific worldview)<br />"Our development is our own consciousness/reason" - idealised version (negativ)<br /><br />In my understanding sofar, He seems here deconstructing everything, He tried excellently to expose our view from cultural attributes, identities and symbols (superstructure : for instance like politics, hierarchies, state, everything which causes division of labour) which un- or intentionally (by nature) formed or established based on economical reason (Basis). Any form of subjugation in history - rulers over masses has appeared due to these 2 factors, which will determine how a society reproduce its own needs and shapes its own history. Regarding establishment and relation between basis and superstructure it is interresting to look in Harmuth Elsenshans' book Kapitalismus Global : Aufstieg - Grenzen - Risiken (2012). How state is established basically to secure its strategic area, therefore state can monopole trade or the emergence of monotheistic religion which basically demanding equality during polytheistic religion was at the time very dominated and hierarchie (division of labour) was so strict (hinduism and another polytheist beliefs). Moreover for Marx&amp;Engels capitalism means accumulation of wealth and we can contemplate it in our current situation - inequality is currently even greater - the richest 1% own the same wealth as the poorest 50% in 2016. Thomas Piketty in his book Capital in 21st also analysed in 2014 great income inequality between workers, managers or CEO from year to year is also even greater.<br />His accumulation theory is also explained well enough by Ibn Khaldun (arabic philosopher in 14. centuries, probably the first arab materialist philosopher) which civilisation makes inequality wider bcs wealth concentrates more on the city and solidarity between tribes has lost.<br /><br />Marxism has heavily shaped my point view how i look and consider society, moreover it was a great experience for me personally to attend (for the first time, since i know Marx's ideas about 5 years ago and read it independently) Marx class in the last semester at my university (probably i wouldn't have opportunity to learn Marx specifically for a semester in my homecountry), as David Harvey said in his class Reading Capital Vol 1 that he has been teaching the capital since long year ago and he always got new interpretation from year to year, so i do, there's a lot of different point of view i have obtained and enlightened me to understand more about the ideas.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12258048987984949411" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2383315413" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2383315413" />
    <a title="EloyVallina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54691559-eloyvallina"><img alt="EloyVallina" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2383315413?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="EloyVallina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="EloyVallina" href="/user/show/54691559-eloyvallina">EloyVallina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780853152170" href="/book/show/797497.German_Ideology">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2383315413" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11836296612891641363">This abridged version of The German Ideology is the first serious text about Marxism I have ever read. Despite some of the philosophical disquisitions being lost on me, as well as some of the references to other authors and historical events, it has been a very enjoyable experience and a good introduction to the subject, which is very much helped by the editor's preface. This is what I made of the book:<br/><br/>According to Marx, the basis of all history is that of the material conditions present at a gi</span>
  <span id="freeText11836296612891641363" style="display:none">This abridged version of The German Ideology is the first serious text about Marxism I have ever read. Despite some of the philosophical disquisitions being lost on me, as well as some of the references to other authors and historical events, it has been a very enjoyable experience and a good introduction to the subject, which is very much helped by the editor's preface. This is what I made of the book:<br /><br />According to Marx, the basis of all history is that of the material conditions present at a given point in time; a society's <i>mode of production</i> is what truly explains its structure, its organisation and its institutions. In that way, it debunks the notion that ideological changes can explain the course of history. It is, he argues, the material reality of things that explains the evolution of thought and so, <i>the driving force of history is not criticism but revolution</i>. Moreover, at any time in History, the ruling ideas have simply being the ideas of the ruling class and its dominance, and so are tainted by the instinct of self-preservation of that class. <br /><br />A leitmotif throughout the whole book is the role of private property and division of labour as the chief forces of history. Actually, in Marx's theory, these are simply two sides of the same coin: <i>in the one, the same thing is affirmed with reference to activity, as is affirmed in the other with reference to the product of the activity</i>. Along History, division of labour has always increased and so it has become the main obstacle for the realisation of people. In particular, the disposed suffer this division of labour as an imposition, alien to them, and which they cannot control or escape, since the only thing they have, their sole livelihood, is precisely their labour. <br /><br />One thing that struck me as surprising was that, rather than promoting or advocating for a revolution against the society of his time, Marx's seems to present this revolution as an inevitable product of the course of history. In his view, there are two practical conditions for the communist revolution: (i) that the situation becomes intolerable for a large number of propertyless people, who would live in contradiction with a world of wealth and culture; and (ii) that the development of the productive forces, that have created these disposed masses, has reached universal proportions. In fact, Marx and Engels seem to believe that the revolution was imminent: <i>We call communism the real movement which abolishes the present state of things. The conditions of this movement result from the premises now in existence</i>.<br /><br />Clearly, the world has changed a lot since 1857, but the revolution didn't quite happen. Or rather, the many attempts that took place during the 2oth century were always thwarted. Perhaps the truth is that we are not all that uncomfortable under capitalism any more, thanks to the many reforms that socialism has brought about. Rosa Luxemburg said: <i>This wall [capitalism] is not overthrown, but is on the contrary strengthened and consolidated by the development of social reforms and the course of democracy</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11836296612891641363" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2686330387" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2686330387" />
    <a title="Kevin Q" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1613737-kevin-q"><img alt="Kevin Q" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2686330387?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin Q" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin Q" href="/user/show/1613737-kevin-q">Kevin Q</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/30528533-the-german-ideology">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2686330387" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15047754936353853798">I wrote notes on this for someone, maybe someone will find them helpful. <br/><br/><br/><u>What is this essay</u><br/>This essay is Marx drawing distinctions between “materialism” and “ideology”. Marx’s theory is borne out of a critique of Hegel’s dialectical idealism. Hegel argued that all art, religion, and culture is moving on an intractable path towards ideal rationality. For Hegel ideas fight each other and the best idea wins and then everyone eventually loves the idea. Hegel points to the enlightenment as the big up</span>
  <span id="freeText15047754936353853798" style="display:none">I wrote notes on this for someone, maybe someone will find them helpful. <br /><br /><br /><u>What is this essay</u><br />This essay is Marx drawing distinctions between “materialism” and “ideology”. Marx’s theory is borne out of a critique of Hegel’s dialectical idealism. Hegel argued that all art, religion, and culture is moving on an intractable path towards ideal rationality. For Hegel ideas fight each other and the best idea wins and then everyone eventually loves the idea. Hegel points to the enlightenment as the big upsurge of rationality using the french revolution and the spread of parliamentary democracy to argue his point. <br /><br /><u>Historical Contextualization</u><br />This essay is written against the prevailing tendency of the Young Hegelians (historical idealists). This essay helps a reader understand what the “materialism” portion of the title “historical materialism” is. Through that elucidation the reader also comes to understand why historical materialism is “historical”. After understanding both these ideas the reader can come to understand what makes HisMat “scientific” as opposed to utopian. <br /><br /><u>Why its awesome</u><br />It is awesome because Hegel founds his analysis upon firmly established Cartesian presumptions. Particularly DesCartes’ assumption that “I think therefore I am”. Squarely placing consciousness a priori to being. <br />This means that most major strands of philosophy after DesCartes, i.e. all enlightenment philosophy and all european philosophy, are predicated upon a huge mistake: the idea that consciousness determines reality. <br />It is laughable but DesCartes’ arguments are predicted upon the idea that consciousness determines nature/reality. As opposed to the obviously more true idea that nature/reality determines consciousness!<br /><br /><u>Epistemological/ontological grounding (why HisMat makes less presumptions)</u><br />If you believe that the world and circumstances you are born in to shape how you think; rather than believing that you thinking reality to be one way MAKES reality that way then guess what: you are agreeing with Marx’s analysis of how we know what we know. <br /><br /><u>Extrapolation from epistemological/ontological grounding</u><br />If nature determines consciousness then the epistemological/ontological grounding of any theory based in Cartesianism is rendered asinine. One has to believe, in some kind of parody of Scientology’s esoteric truths, that just thinking something causes reality to change in order to conform with your will/minds eye construction.<br /><br /><u>Ok that’s how history has worked, So what’s the issue?</u><br />The issue is firstly that most western thought has remained mired in Cartesianism which means only theory which is based on the idea of consciousness determining nature gets published and discussed in most academia. So the first point is that most history and philosophy is engaging in dialectical idealism (Hegelian idealism/historical idealism). When Marx is talking trash about the Young Hegelians he is talking trash about ALL historical idealists. Secondly is the way to escape this mess. If nature determines consciousness, but humans are a kind of peculiar type of animal that can make conscious(i.e. deliberate/intentional) changes to its environment….then humans can alter reality in a way that creates certain modes of consciousness. <br /><br /><u>Over a long period of time</u><br />We are convinced that only ideas can change reality, when it is reality that needs changing in order to alter ideas. We can never know exactly the perfect way to change reality (because the past nature is always effecting how we are conscious of reality we cannot ever perfectly arrive at pure knowledge). But we can guess and check. We can act on reality/materiality (praxis/practice), then analyze the effects of our action on consciousness (theoria/theory), and then alter our practice based on our critique of how it effected our consciousness (aufheben/sublation/synthesis). <br />This method of historical materialism (theory, practice, synthesis) is taken directly from Hegel’s historical idealism but is focused on changes to material reality as opposed to changes in mindsets. <br /><br /><u>So why is it Historical Materialism?</u><br />The materialism part is that it believes nature determines consciousness as opposed to consciousness determining nature. <br />The historical part is that this is never a snapshot of how nature determines consciousness, but instead takes the form of a narrative which situates a phenomenon within a larger scope of understanding (human society as a whole instead of any one specific sociological abstraction like culture, art, religion, etc.).<br />Scientific HisMat argues for analysis of our actions following them through to how they might “determine the consciousness of [wo]men” it also analyzes the surface phenomena more effectively by tying the phenomena back to the material reality that produced said phenomena. <br />By examining changes to material and the effects on consciousness throughout history, we can improve our interventions in to material reality. Until we end up in a situation where nobody has their labor stolen (communism). This method is what makes HisMat scientific.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15047754936353853798" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2586577847" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2586577847" />
    <a title="Slava Skobeloff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48090404-slava-skobeloff"><img alt="Slava Skobeloff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548177869p2/48090404.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2586577847?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Slava Skobeloff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Slava Skobeloff" href="/user/show/48090404-slava-skobeloff">Slava Skobeloff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780717803019" href="/book/show/10494763-german-ideology">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48090404-slava-skobeloff?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2586577847" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16230062517981362364">Finished it today just this morning. What it honestly feels like is a mix between the Communist Manifesto (in talking about the necessity of revolution against the current state of affairs) and Das Kapital (it repeats a lot of the same points about the consolidation of industry that Capital also talks about, albeit in much less detail).<br/><br/>The problem is that a lot of people call this a good introduction to Marxist theory but I'm afraid I just can't see that. A lot of the book feels like very abstra</span>
  <span id="freeText16230062517981362364" style="display:none">Finished it today just this morning. What it honestly feels like is a mix between the Communist Manifesto (in talking about the necessity of revolution against the current state of affairs) and Das Kapital (it repeats a lot of the same points about the consolidation of industry that Capital also talks about, albeit in much less detail).<br /><br />The problem is that a lot of people call this a good introduction to Marxist theory but I'm afraid I just can't see that. A lot of the book feels like very abstract generalisations that if you don't already have a good grasp on Marx's ideas then it can be pretty hard to follow. It's true that he introduces some basic ideas about base/superstructure, ideology, criticism of German idealism and the forms of intercourse, but rarely goes into deep detail about any of it. It would be much better to read Capital instead if you want to have a better and more full understanding of Marxist theory.<br /><br />Part A, 'Idealism and Materialism' deals with Marx's objection to the Young Hegelians and their 'idealist' worldview that ignores the actual material conditions of existence. It's pretty straightfoward although does require you to know a bit of context--in particular Hegel's movement of World-Spirit or Stirner's conception of the 'Unique'. Other than that it's a pretty solid section.<br /><br />Part B, 'Illusion of the Epoch' discusses mainly the famous dichotomy between 'base' and 'superstructure' (Marx doesn't actually use those words). Aside from the weird critique of Feuerbach smack in the middle, it provides an interesting discussion of the formation of society and its ruling ideology.<br /><br />Part C, 'The Real Basis of Ideology' highlights the historical development of the division of labour, the rise of manufacture and the relationship of the government to the bourgeoisie. Again, this is the part where it helps if you've read Capital, since basically a lot of the sections near the end of the book (Capital) repeat the same themes as stated here but in a more comprehensible and detailed form.<br /><br />Part D, 'Proletarians and Communists' essentially states that the contradictions between the proletarian and the bourgeoisie that arise from the division of labour produced by the contradiction between the form of intercourse and the productive forces are what call for a revolution. For some reason it feels very abstractly written and doesn't have a lot of elaboration on some of its points (i.e it talks about how the transition from feudalism to capitalism occurred via this contradiction, but this contradiction isn't very evident how it developed). <br /><br />The rest of the book is a critique of Bruno Bauer and Max Stirner which aren't helpful unless you've read their respective works. I've never read Bauer so I cannot comment on him, as for Stirner, one would be better off not really reading him and just glancing through the SEP page or Wikipedia. Stirner's generally not very popular in academia because of his support of the widely discredited psychological egoism and really is only very studied in niche anarchist communities online. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16230062517981362364" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Aman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/91616693-aman"><img alt="Aman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582703895p2/91616693.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780717803019" href="/book/show/10494763-german-ideology">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

<span id="freeTextContainer6275538622608838941">Reading the abridged version as a companion to the full version. Definitely useful, as I wanted a basis more substantial than the <i>Manifesto</i> going into <i>Capital</i> (beyond the prefaces, which are valuable in their own right). <br/><br/>Marx's critique of the German idealists (is savage) argues that they often take at face value the ideas, concepts, morality, etc. of their given era, which are rather "directly interwoven with the material activity and the material intercourse of men [...] as they are conditione</span>
  <span id="freeText6275538622608838941" style="display:none">Reading the abridged version as a companion to the full version. Definitely useful, as I wanted a basis more substantial than the <i>Manifesto</i> going into <i>Capital</i> (beyond the prefaces, which are valuable in their own right). <br /><br />Marx's critique of the German idealists (is savage) argues that they often take at face value the ideas, concepts, morality, etc. of their given era, which are rather "directly interwoven with the material activity and the material intercourse of men [...] as they are conditioned by a definite development of their productive forces and of the intercourse corresponding to these". When attacking Stirner, he fashions a quotable summary of this critique: <blockquote>It is to be noted here, as in general with ideologists, that they inevitably put a thing upside-down and regard their ideology both as the creative force and as the aim of all social relations, whereas it is only an expression and symptom of these relations.</blockquote> The focus on history, in refuting the ideations of the Saints, situates the development and rise of bourgeois logic. This is the logic which underlies our law and morality, our politics, and our media ("it is easier to imagine an end to the world than an end to capitalism" Jameson and/or Žižek). <br /><br />What is (roughly) the rise of the bourgeoisie? Once lowly serfs, they were freed from their feudal ties, escaped to the cities, initiated their trades, took apprentices, formed guilds, absorbed freed serfs into their guilds, beefed heavily with the feudal lords, manufacturing rose, and the bourgeois continued to accumulate capital. Eventually, demand surpassed the capabilities of the existing productive forces, and what laid bare was the necessity of big industry onward.<br /><br />Overall, I definitely need to read the full version (after <i>Capital</i>) in its entirely, including the "Introduction to the Critique of Political Economy".</span>
  <a data-text-id="6275538622608838941" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1297263845" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1297263845" />
    <a title="G.D. Master" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42599940-g-d-master"><img alt="G.D. Master" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430253297p2/42599940.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1297263845?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="G.D. Master" class="user" itemprop="url" name="G.D. Master" href="/user/show/42599940-g-d-master">G.D. Master</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781614270485" href="/book/show/11319144-the-german-ideology">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Academics, economists, philosophers, and social sciences</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42599940-g-d-master?shelf=academic">academic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42599940-g-d-master?shelf=industrial-novel">industrial-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42599940-g-d-master?shelf=graphite-drug">graphite-drug</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42599940-g-d-master?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1297263845" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12227249011899755793">Written after Engels’s work “The Condition of the Working Class in England,” “The German Ideology” is a reaction to Ludwig Feuerbach and G. W. F. Hegel. It is not a direct reaction, but gathers the important parts of their philosophies and elaborates on them for a blending with the ideas of Engels and Marx. “The German Ideology” is an important work because it attempts to solve the philosophical problem of other minds. Philosophy often deals with individual reasoning and to further extent metaph</span>
  <span id="freeText12227249011899755793" style="display:none">Written after Engels’s work “The Condition of the Working Class in England,” “The German Ideology” is a reaction to Ludwig Feuerbach and G. W. F. Hegel. It is not a direct reaction, but gathers the important parts of their philosophies and elaborates on them for a blending with the ideas of Engels and Marx. “The German Ideology” is an important work because it attempts to solve the philosophical problem of other minds. Philosophy often deals with individual reasoning and to further extent metaphysics. Engels and Marx were more interested in political economy and how society could be improved through its practice. In order to begin evolving a grander schema of their improvement of society, they needed to address human rationality in a large social context and develop empirical ways of supporting ideas for their arguments. Using history and politics, Engels and Marx explore how technology, economics, and social class structure have evolved to their nineteenth century state. By understanding how human behavior operates en masse with material wealth and infrastructure Engels and Marx set out to create a dialectical approach to solving economic and social instability. “The German Ideology” is not a completion or comprehensive explanation of their ideology and/or philosophy. It is a theological exploration of their early ideas leading to later works such as “The Communist Manifesto” and Marx’s “Das Kapital.” Again, it is most interesting for its arguments regarding other minds and how the human species works together.<br /><br />Like other works by Engels and Marx, “The German Ideology” has its cynical moments and jabs at people and social institutions held in low esteem by Engels and Marx. This is not unusual. Most literature is slanted toward an author’s beliefs. Like other works by these two literary icons, their satire is laughable and at times thought provoking when not overly critical of people’s backgrounds. Their writing, like Engels’s in “The Condition of the Working Class,” consists of long sentences built with clauses and strategic punctuation. Readers with well-organized thought processes will enjoy the work. It is not written with recreational readers in mind. It is an intellectually challenging work for academics and professionals in such fields as philosophy, social science, politics, and economics.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12227249011899755793" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2837055760" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2837055760" />
    <a title="Daniel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2404888-daniel"><img alt="Daniel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559046030p2/2404888.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2837055760?book_show_action=true">Jun 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel" href="/user/show/2404888-daniel">Daniel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780853152170" href="/book/show/797497.German_Ideology">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2837055760" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4684752622440568123">Marx’s Critique of the German Ideology is one of the most important texts he wrote, though never published during his life. It offers the most comprehensive description of his historical materialist conception of history, as well as his most thorough critique of the idea-driven history that had always dominated European thinking and that was particularly influential in Germany. The Critique is in fact a portfolio of critiques, somewhat messy in their structure and organization, on the Young Hege</span>
  <span id="freeText4684752622440568123" style="display:none">Marx’s Critique of the German Ideology is one of the most important texts he wrote, though never published during his life. It offers the most comprehensive description of his historical materialist conception of history, as well as his most thorough critique of the idea-driven history that had always dominated European thinking and that was particularly influential in Germany. The Critique is in fact a portfolio of critiques, somewhat messy in their structure and organization, on the Young Hegelians. The important section—the only one worth reading, really—is the first one, “Feuerbach: Opposition of the Materialist and Idealist Outlooks.”<br /><br />In an opening PREFACE, Marx states his purpose: to debunk the “chimeras” and “childlike fancies” that are “the kernel of the modern Young-Hegelian philosophy—of “uncloaking these sheep, who take themselves and are taken for wolves.” <br /><br />In SECTION A, he (1) attacks the Young Hegelian’s idealism, (2) outlines the fundamental premises of his materialist conception of history, and (3) makes some rather extravagant claims about how life under communism will be like when man is free to dedicate himself to “all-round development” rather than degrading specialization. The Young Hegelians reject the theological dimensions of Hegel’s philosophy, arguing that “end of history” is not in fact embodied in the liberal democratic Prussian state, as the Old Hegelians thought, because the state is founded on theologically grounded and therefore morally erroneous premises. Yet, while the Young Hegelians sought to secularize Hegel’s thinking, they agreed with their “master’s” fundamental assumption that history is essentially idea-driven--that it’s, to be more specific, shaped by a dialectical play between opposing ideas. For the Hegelians, young and old alike, it was ideas that “made things happen,” and the deepest, most fundamental determining factor, for the way history turned out was the set of ideas that practically predetermined history. Marx’s dismisses this ideological conception of history with acid scorn, deriding it as “philosophic charlatanry.” He accuses the Young Hegelians debates of consisting in “empty fighting against phrases,” and this is because they fail to realize, in his view, the fundamental determining force of history, which is material rather than ideology. The premises of history, according to Marx, “are men, not in any fantastic isolation and rigidity, but in their actual, empirically perceptible process of development under definite conditions.” The most fundamental conditions of human existence are economic for Marx—the conditions under which we are able to produce the necessary basics of human life (food, water, shelter, clothing). Therefore, it is only by examining a society’s “mode of production” and the “relations of production” this gives rise to that one can engage with the empirical material reality of that society; it’s this material reality, in turn, that shapes culture, ideology, history, philosophy, and consciousness itself. All of these are the “superstructure” of the economic “base.” <br /><br />“In direct contrast to German philosophy, which descends from heaven to earth, here we ascend from earth to heaven. That is to say, we do not set out from what men say, imagine, conceive, nor from men as narrated, thought of, imagined, conceived, in order to arrive at man in the flesh. We set out from real active men, and on the basis of their real-life process we demonstrate the development of the ideological reflexes and echoes of this life-process. The phantoms formed in the human brain are also, necessarily, sublimates of their material life-process, which is empirically verifiable and bound to material premises. Morality, religion, metaphysics, all the rest of ideology and their corresponding forms of consciousness, thus no longer retain any semblance of independence. They have no history, no development; but men, developing their material production and material intercourse, altar, along with their real existence, their thinking and the products of their thinking. Life is not determined by consciousness, but consciousness by life.”<br /><br />Of fundamental importance to Marx are the questions of HOW labor is divided among the members of a community, HOW goods are (usually unequally) distributed, and HOW this division of labor and unequal distribution shapes relationships within society. It’s these “relations of production” and the KIND of civil society that produce that are at the heart of human lived experience and therefore human history. <br /><br />Marx delves into these “primary historical relationships” in a description of the evolution of human consciousness. He identifies the four premises on which history rests: People must be able to produce the means to fulfill their basic needs--to eat, drink, find shelter, clothing, etc. This is the first “historical act.” The second is the satisfaction of these needs. The third historical act is the propagation of the species: men and women must produce children to continue humankind. Fourthly, people must enter into a cooperative social relationship with one another, if only to help them achieve the first three goals. It’s the need to communicate with others that is responsible for emergence of language and what Marx describes as a primitive kind of consciousness “concerning the immediate sensuous environment and consciousness of the limited connection with other persons and things outside the individual who is growing self-conscious.” As we move away from tribal societies a foundational division between physical and mental labor emerges. “From this moment onwards consciousness can really flatter itself that it is something other than consciousness of existing practice, that it really represents something without representing something real.” This is only an illusion, however, as all “Ideology” is simply an echo of a society’s material conditions.<br /><br />Over the course of history, the way labor is organized and divided among members of a community generates a number of “contradictions” (between enjoyment and labor, production and consumption, individual and communal interest, one’s nation’s economic circumstances and another’s). These contradictions fuel the class consciousness, than in a sufficiently developed form, ignite the revolutionary sentiments that lead to revolution. So for Marx, history is the story of how one economic “epoch” gives way to another through revolution. Marx’s emphasis on material conditions as the driving force of history pits him against Hegel’s emphasis on ideas. Yet, Marx’s notion that history has an ultimate end or solution is highly Hegelian. And Marx believes (simplistically, I think) that the history of economic epoch giving way to economic epoch would end in a mature communist society—the “end of history” for Marx. <br /><br />In SECTION B, The Illusion of the Epoch, Marx identifies civil society as the “true source and theater of history” because it’s the most fundamental level in which the social relations determined by the relations of production can be discerned. It’s these “real relations” that drive history, not thinking, as Hegel’s acolytes believed. Marx singles out Feuerbach for critique. Feuerbach, Marx asserts, that Hegel was right to think to man as a sensuous being but that he ultimately failed to discern the driving force of history: material conditions, as shaped by the “mode” and “relations of production.” Marx finishes with a chapter on the “ruling class and ruling ideas,” in which he argues that “ruling ideas are nothing more than the ideal expression of the dominant material relationships… grasped as ideas.” “For instance,” he continues, “in an age and in a country where royal power, aristocracy, and bourgeoisie are contending for mastery and where, therefore, mastery is shared, the doctrine of the separation of powers proves to be the dominant idea and is expressed as an “eternal law”… during the time that the aristocracy was dominant, the concepts honour, loyaly, etc. were dominant, during the dominance of the bourgeoisie the concepts freedom, equality.” It is the thinkers of the ruling class (the ideology producers, in the broadest sense) that elaborate the ideal forms or illusions of about their age, with its distinct economic conditions. These are then given a “form of universality”; they are represented as “the only rational, universally valid ones.” Marx asserts that it will no longer be essential to “represent a particular interest as a general interest” under communism. German philosophy is based on the illusion that it innovates in the realm of the idea, whereas, as Marx sees it, it is merely reproduces the ideological clichés that arise from its economic conditions.<br /><br />In SECTION C, The Real Basis of Ideology, Marx outlines four major epochs in the history of the economic conditions: tribal society, slavery, feudalism, and capitalism. He acknowledges that each of these periods has its own micro-history and patterns of development. Yet for the purpose of simplification and to make his point he simplifies history into these four major strata. Tribal societies, with their underdeveloped stage of production and elementary division of labor, have largely “communal” relations; they embody a kind of “primitive communism.” Slavery is characterized by the most oppressive division between power and labor. The straightforward exploitation of slavery is more concealed under feudalism by an array of useful ideological illusions. Peasants are given their plot of land in an attempt to incentivize them to work for their superiors (what incentived slave labor except fear of one’s master?), and there is a well-established mythology of interdependent and divinely sanctioned feudal relations between peasants, lords, noblemen, and monarchs—a mythology that softens and obscures what is ultimately an brutally inegalitarian arrangement of labor and privilege. Still, Marx seems to feel that people had the opportunity for a more wholesome beginning-to-end kind of labor (as in the guild system, where one sees a product through every stage of production) that allowed people to take pride in their work. Finally, there’s capitalism. Its extremely sophisticated division of labor produces enormous wealth, but this is unequally distributed and the highly specialized nature of work degrades human life and produces a profound experience of alienation in workers. <br /><br />In SECTION D, PROLETARIANS AND COMMUNISM, Marx descirbes the conditions in which individuals will relate to eachother as ''complete'' individuals under communism. This section speculates about the social relations that will emerge under communism. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4684752622440568123" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2742209267?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter Harrison" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter Harrison" href="/user/show/24002867-peter-harrison">Peter Harrison</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780853152170" href="/book/show/797497.German_Ideology">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2742209267" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10646893038052111172">This selection carves out the most section of "The German Ideology" which gives the most complete outline of Marx's theory of history. In contrast to the common conception (coloured by the dialectical materialism of the Stalinist era) this theory is not a mechanical deterministic economism. Marx does though connect the workings of the economy to the workings of society in a way that is much more subtle than many of his subsequent disciples have followed.<br/><br/>It was reading segments of this book in Da</span>
  <span id="freeText10646893038052111172" style="display:none">This selection carves out the most section of "The German Ideology" which gives the most complete outline of Marx's theory of history. In contrast to the common conception (coloured by the dialectical materialism of the Stalinist era) this theory is not a mechanical deterministic economism. Marx does though connect the workings of the economy to the workings of society in a way that is much more subtle than many of his subsequent disciples have followed.<br /><br />It was reading segments of this book in David McClellan's "Selected Writings" as a student that first switched me on to Marx's analysis, and for me it remains a persuasive book. It strikes me that Marx is not dogmatic, making the case for the sort of detailed and empirical work championed by historians such as EP Thompson, but founded in an analysis of class struggle and the relations of production.<br /><br />This book is much shorter than the original, leaving out most of the second section which is by all accounts a line by line attack on Max Stirner, a contemporary German writer. It does though include the Theses on Feuerbach, which at the end of the books feels like a suitable bookmark to Marx's thought in this period, ending with the call to philosophers to change the world rather than merely interpret it.<br /><br />I mention some of Marx's thinking on class consciousness in my recent blog post on Brexit, based on articles by John Harris and Aditya Chakrabortty here:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://marxadventure.wordpress.com/2019/03/17/brexit-and-ideology">https://marxadventure.wordpress.com/2...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10646893038052111172" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2472631282" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2472631282" />
    <a title="Dierk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/75002118-dierk"><img alt="Dierk" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2472631282?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dierk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dierk" href="/user/show/75002118-dierk">Dierk</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2472631282" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer460944747559401594">The German Ideology is Marx's polemic against some of his notable German and French contemporaries of the 1840s. <br/><br/>Until the 1930s, it only existed in manuscript form. Whether Marx ever wanted it to be published and whether he would have done more work on the manuscript in that case are unclear. Even in its final rough form, however, The German Ideology provides valuable insights into Marx's researching, thinking and writing styles.<br/><br/>To appreciate this book fully, the reader should be familiar with:</span>
  <span id="freeText460944747559401594" style="display:none">The German Ideology is Marx's polemic against some of his notable German and French contemporaries of the 1840s. <br /><br />Until the 1930s, it only existed in manuscript form. Whether Marx ever wanted it to be published and whether he would have done more work on the manuscript in that case are unclear. Even in its final rough form, however, The German Ideology provides valuable insights into Marx's researching, thinking and writing styles.<br /><br />To appreciate this book fully, the reader should be familiar with: very obscure writers, the intellectual scene in Europe during the 1840s and Marx's polemical style. I may revisit it after acquiring some of these prerequisites because it is said to reveal important information about the later direction of Marx's thought. <br /><br />A first read-through, even without any context, shows how Marx is willing to 'go to the mat' to expose what he considers logical errors. His takedowns tend to be quite thorough. He enumerates ALL the ways he disagrees with someone (instead of settling for just one or two). A consistently sarcastic and derisive tone is another essential element of this manuscript. <br /><br />Summary: not the best introduction to Marx's oeuvre but still interesting and worth reconsidering after acquiring enough background.</span>
  <a data-text-id="460944747559401594" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_58378250" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58378250" />
    <a title="Omar Mastroianni" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2380183-omar-mastroianni"><img alt="Omar Mastroianni" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1244091603p2/2380183.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/58378250?book_show_action=true">Jun 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Omar Mastroianni" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Omar Mastroianni" href="/user/show/2380183-omar-mastroianni">Omar Mastroianni</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58378250" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1215536458522679234">In which Marx discounts the logical Nihilism of Max Stirner. The ivory tower thought allowing Stirner to muse on the mind and wax solipsistic while the world in reality is crumbling around those who don't have time to "spout wise" and are desperately trying to make ends meet, (or are exploiting those trying to do so) is the sobriety test which Stirner,& all philosophy, fail if historical facticity is not front & center in an honest analysis. For his sabbatical from such mundane things as reality</span>
  <span id="freeText1215536458522679234" style="display:none">In which Marx discounts the logical Nihilism of Max Stirner. The ivory tower thought allowing Stirner to muse on the mind and wax solipsistic while the world in reality is crumbling around those who don't have time to "spout wise" and are desperately trying to make ends meet, (or are exploiting those trying to do so) is the sobriety test which Stirner,&amp; all philosophy, fail if historical facticity is not front &amp; center in an honest analysis. For his sabbatical from such mundane things as reality, Marx dubs Stirner "St.Max",as bourgeois in his earnestness as the monks . Yet his poetic reasoning prematurely withdraws from the problem of life, in that it gives up hope of mankind achieving a fraternal sense of respect &amp; meaning,not to mention survival.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1215536458522679234" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('YfC9TDBHxKqH1i5HGPm/0B+tcF5OswUXkUf8vAbVaarL71aWwgmR3z/WAcO9Dc+cxF/z6i2S0bsyXB6hEcYgOw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

The history of the family dates from 1861, the year of the publication of Bachofen\'s Mutterrecht (maternal law) Engles makes the following propositions: 1. That in the beginning people lived in unrestricted sexual intercourse, which he dubs, not very felicitously, hetaerism. 2. That such an intercourse excludes any absolutely certain means of determining parentage; that consequently descent could only be traced by the female line in compliance with maternal law; and that this was universally practiced by all the nations of antiquity. 3. That consequently women as mothers, being the only well known parents of younger generations, received a high tribute of respect and deference, amounting to a complete women\'s rule (gynaicocracy), according to Bachofen\'s idea. 4. That the transition to monogamy, reserving a certain woman exclusively to one man, implied the violation of the primeval religious law (i.e., practically a violation of the customary right of all other men to the same woman), which violation had to be atoned for its permission purchased by the surrender of the women to the public for a limited time.<\/span>\n  <a <script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_18310364'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18310364-ideology-and-ideological-state-apparatuses?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Ideology and Ideological State Apparatuses<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/49146.Louis_Althusser?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Louis Althusser<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.10 avg rating &mdash; 868 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1970\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer6673702290450213437\">Louis Althusser’s renowned short text ‘Ideology and Ideological State Apparatuses’ radically transformed the concept of the subject, the understanding of the state and even the very frameworks of cultural, political and literary theory. The text has <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6673702290450213437\" style=\"display:none\">Louis Althusser’s renowned short text ‘Ideology and Ideological State Apparatuses’ radically transformed the concept of the subject, the understanding of the state and even the very frameworks of cultural, political and literary theory. The text has influenced thinkers such as Judith Butler, Ernesto Laclau and Slavoj Žižek.\n\nThe piece is, in fact, an extract from a much longer book, On the Reproduction of Capitalism, until now unavailable in English. Its publication makes possible a reappraisal of seminal Althusserian texts already available in English, their place in Althusser’s oeuvre and the relevance of his ideas for contemporary theory. On the Reproduction of Capitalism develops Althusser’s conception of historical materialism, outlining the conditions of reproduction in capitalist society and the revolutionary struggle for its overthrow.\n\nWritten in the afterglow of May 1968, the text addresses a question that continues to haunt us today: in a society that proclaims its attachment to the ideals of liberty and equality, why do we witness the ever-renewed reproduction of relations of domination? Both a conceptually innovative text and a key theoretical tool for activists, On the Reproduction of Capitalism is an essential addition to the corpus of the twentieth-century Left.<\/span>\n  <a Manchester<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText2243610631984397788\" style=\"display:none\">The Condition of the Working Class is the best-known work of Engels, and in many ways still the best study of the working class in Victorian England. It was also Engels\'s first book, written during his stay in Manchester from 1842 to 1844. Manchester was then at the very heart of the Industrial Revolution and Engels compiled his study from his own observations and detailed contemporary reports. The fluency of his writing, the personal nature of his insights, and his talent for mordant satire combine to make this account of the life of the victims of early industrial change into a classic - a historical study that parallels and complements the fictional works of the time by such writers as Gaskell and Dickens. What Cobbett had done for agricultural poverty in his Rural Rides, Engels did - and more - in this work on the plight of the industrial workers in the England of the early 1840s. //<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_74176'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/74176.The_Protestant_Ethic_and_the_Spirit_of_Capitalism?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/42041.Max_Weber?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Max Weber<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.90 avg rating &mdash; 10,791 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1904\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer5351456723429661177\">The Protestant ethic — a moral code stressing hard work, rigorous self-discipline, and the organization of one\'s life in the service of God — was made famous by sociologist and political economist Max Weber. In this brilliant study (his best-known an<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText5351456723429661177\" style=\"display:none\">The Protestant ethic — a moral code stressing hard work, rigorous self-discipline, and the organization of one\'s life in the service of God — was made famous by sociologist and political economist Max Weber. In this brilliant study (his best-known and most controversial), he opposes the Marxist concept of dialectical materialism and its view that change takes place through &quot;the struggle of opposites.&quot; Instead, he relates the rise of a capitalist economy to the Puritan determination to work out anxiety over salvation or damnation by performing good deeds — an effort that ultimately discouraged belief in predestination and encouraged capitalism. Weber\'s classic study has long been required reading in college and advanced high school social studies classrooms.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"5351456723429661177\" href=\"#\" For Marx, first published in France in 1968, has come to be regarded as the founding text of the school of “structuralist Marxism” which was presided over by the fascinating and enigmatic figure of Louis Althusser. Structuralism constituted an intellectual revolution in the 1960s and 1970s and radically transformed the way philosophy, political and social theory, history, science, and aesthetics were discussed and thought about. For Marx was a key contribution to that process and it fundamentally recast the way in which many people understood Marx and Marxism.\n\nThis book contains the classic statements of Althusser\'s analysis of the young Marx and the importance of Feuerbach during this formative period, of his thesis of the “epistomological break” between the early and the late Marx, and of his conception of dialectics, contradiction and “overdetermination.” Also included is a study of the materialist theater of Bertolazzi and Brecht and the critique of humanist readings of Marxism. Since his death in 1990, Althusser\'s legacy has come under renewed examination and it is increasingly recognized that the influence of his ideas has been wider and deeper than previously thought: He also maps out the external and internal forces that destroyed communism, and the disastrous impact of the “free-market” victory on eastern Europe and the former Soviet Union. He affirms the relevance of taboo ideologies like Marxism, demonstrating the importance of class analysis in understanding political realities and dealing with the ongoing collision between ecology and global corporatism.\n\nWritten with lucid and compelling style, this book goes beyond truncated modes of thought, inviting us to entertain iconoclastic views, and to ask why things are as they are. It is a bold and entertaining exploration of the epic struggles of yesterday and today.\n\n&quot;A penetrating and persuasive writer with an astonishing array of documentation to implement his attacks.&quot;—The Catholic Journalist\n\n&quot;Blackshirts &amp; Reds discusses the great combat between fascism and socialism that is the defining feature of the Twentieth Century, and takes every official version to task for its substitution of moral analysis for critical analysis, for its selectivity, and for its errata. By portraying the struggle between fascism and Communism in this century as a single conflict, and not a series of discrete encounters, between the insatiable need for new capital on the one hand and the survival of a system under siege on the other, Parenti defines fascism as the weapon of capitalism, not simply an extreme form of it. Fascism is not an aberration, he points out, but a &quot;rational&quot; and integral component of the system.&quot;—Stan Goff, The Prism\n\nMichael Parenti, PhD Yale, is an internationally known author and lecturer. He is one of the nation\'s leadiing progressive political analysts. He is the author of over 275 published articles and twenty books. His writings are published in popular periodicals, scholarly journals, and his op-ed pieces have been in leading newspapers such as The New York Times and The Los Angeles Times. Written during the Second World War and circulated privately, it appeared in a printed edition in Amsterdam in 1947. \"What we had s<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText9756216565828156747\" style=\"display:none\">Dialectic of Enlightenment is undoubtedly the most influential publication of the Frankfurt School of Critical Theory. Written during the Second World War and circulated privately, it appeared in a printed edition in Amsterdam in 1947. &quot;What we had set out to do,&quot; the authors write in the Preface, &quot;was nothing less than to explain why humanity, instead of entering a truly human state, is sinking into a new kind of barbarism.&quot;Yet the work goes far beyond a mere critique of contemporary events. Historically remote developments, indeed, the birth of Western history and of subjectivity itself out of the struggle against natural forces, as represented in myths, are connected in a wide arch to the most threatening experiences of the present. The book consists in five chapters, at first glance unconnected, together with a number of shorter notes. The various analyses concern such phenomena as the detachment of science from practical life, formalized morality, the manipulative nature of entertainment culture, and a paranoid behavioral structure, expressed in aggressive anti-Semitism, that marks the limits of enlightenment. The authors perceive a common element in these phenomena, the tendency toward self-destruction of the guiding criteria inherent in enlightenment thought from the beginning. Using historical analyses to elucidate the present, they show, against the background of a prehistory of subjectivity, why the National Socialist terror was not an aberration of modern history but was rooted deeply in the fundamental characteristics of Western civilization.Adorno and Horkheimer see the self-destruction of Western reason as grounded in a historical and fateful dialectic between the domination of external nature and society. They trace enlightenment, which split these spheres apart, back to its mythical roots. Enlightenment and myth, therefore, are not irreconcilable opposites, but dialectically mediated qualities of both real and intellectual life. &quot;Myth is already enlightenment, and enlightenment reverts to mythology.&quot; This paradox is the fundamental thesis of the book.This new translation, based on the text in the complete edition of the works of Max Horkheimer, contains textual variants, commentary upon them, and an editorial discussion of the position of this work in the development of Critical Theory.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"9756216565828156747\" href=\"#\" <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6329598-the-foundations-of-leninism"><img alt="The Foundations of Leninism" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1236836541l/6329598._SX98_.jpg" />

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_6329598'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6329598-the-foundations-of-leninism?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Foundations of Leninism<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/138332.Joseph_Stalin?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Joseph Stalin<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.96 avg rating &mdash; 499 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1965\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer3021963008953685158\">\"Los fundamentos del leninismo: el tema es vasto. Para agotarlo, haria falta un libro entero. Mas aun: haria falta toda una serie de libros. Por eso es natural que mis conferencias no puedan ser consideradas como una exposicion completa del leninismo<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText3021963008953685158\" style=\"display:none\">&quot;Los fundamentos del leninismo: el tema es vasto. Para agotarlo, haria falta un libro entero. Mas aun: haria falta toda una serie de libros. Por eso es natural que mis conferencias no puedan ser consideradas como una exposicion completa del leninismo. Seran tan solo, en el mejor de los casos, un resumen sucinto de los fundamentos del leninismo. No obstante, estimo util hacer este resumen, a fin de ofrecer algunos puntos fundamentales de partida, necesarios para estudiar con fruto el leninismo. Exponer los fundamentos del leninismo no es aun exponer los fundamentos de la concepcion del mundo de Lenin. La concepcion del mundo de Lenin y los fundamentos del leninismo no son, por su volumen, una y la misma cosa. Lenin es marxista, y la base de su concepcion del mundo es, naturalmente, el marxismo. Pero de esto no se desprende, en modo alguno, que la exposicion del leninismo deba comenzar por la de los fundamentos del marxismo. Exponer el leninismo es exponer lo que hay de peculiar y de nuevo en las obras de Lenin, lo aportado por Lenin al tesoro general del marxismo y lo que esta asociado a su nombre de modo natural. Solo en este sentido hablare en mis conferencias de los fundamentos del leninismo.&quot;--- Josef Stalin<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"3021963008953685158\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer wtrSignedOut\' id=\'24_book_6329598\'>\n<div class=\'wtrUp wtrLeft\'>\n<form action=\"/shelf/add_to_shelf\"
